حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت عبداللہ ابن عباس نا پسند کرتے تھے کہ آدمی بیچ کرے پھر اس کو واپس لوٹائے اور اس کے ساتھ کچھ درہم لوٹائے ۔ اور اس میں اشارہ ہے اس بات کا کہ اقالہ فنخ بیج ہے۔ اس لئے نہیں جائز ہے اقالہ مگر راس المال یعنی پہلی قیمت کے ساتھ ۔
Chapter of Iqaalah. Book of Sales. [10] (1) Iqaalah is permissible in sale for the seller and the buyer at the like of the first price. [911] (2) Then if he stipulates more than it, the condition is void and he returns at the like of the first price. The meaning of iqaalah: the seller regrets after selling, asks the buyer to return the sold item and take back the price, and the buyer does so; or the buyer regrets and asks the seller to take back the sold item and give the price. Doing so is permissible, rather preferable. Evidence from hadith: From Abu Huraira, he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a Muslim, Allah will forgive his slips.' (Abu Dawood, Ibn Majah). This hadith indicates iqaalah is permissible and rewardable. [910] (1) Iqaalah is permissible in sale for the seller and the buyer at the like of the first price. The seller returns exactly the price he took. (2) From Ibn Abbas: he disliked that a man sell then return with extra dirhams. This indicates iqaalah is faskh (rescission), so it is not permissible except at the original capital. From Aswad: he disliked returning with extra. Iqaalah is faskh of the first sale. [911] (2) Then if a condition of more or less than the first price is stipulated, the condition is void, and the first price is returned. Example: bought for four hundred, buyer demands five hundred or seller offers three hundred, the condition is void but iqaalah is valid at the first price. Footnote: (a) He said: whoever rescinds for a Muslim, Allah will forgive his sins. (b) Ibn Abbas disliked returning with extra dirhams; this indicates iqaalah is faskh, so not valid except at original capital. (c) Aswad disliked returning with extra. Then: if he stipulates more than it, the condition is void and he returns at the like of the first price. [12] (3) It is faskh for the two contracting parties, a new sale for others, according to Abu Hanifa. [913] (4) Destruction of the price does not prevent validity of iqaalah, and destruction of the sold item prevents its validity. (1914) (5) And if some of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remainder. Commentary: (3) Iqaalah is faskh for the two parties and a new sale for others in the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. The third party sees the sold item moving from buyer to seller with offer and acceptance, so it is like a new sale. Therefore, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) can be claimed. (4) Destruction of price does not prevent iqaalah because price is fungible; destruction of sold item prevents because the item is specific. (5) If part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remaining portion. Example: six kilos of wheat bought for ten rupees; three kilos destroyed; remaining three can be returned for five rupees. Footnote: (a) Ibn Abbas disliked returning with extra; this indicates iqaalah is faskh, not valid except at original capital.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on Iqaalah (rescission of sale) in Kitab al-Buyoo'. The author begins by defining iqaalah: it is when either the seller or the buyer regrets the transaction and the other party agrees to annul it. The first ruling states that iqaalah is permissible (ja'iz) and even preferable (afdal), provided it is done at the original price (bi-mithl al-thaman al-awwal). The proof is a hadith: 'Whoever rescinds for a Muslim, Allah will rescind his sins.' This is a strong encouragement. Then an athar from Ibn Abbas shows that returning the sold item with extra money is disliked, indicating that iqaalah is a rescission (faskh), not a new sale, so no extra payment is allowed.

The second ruling addresses a stipulation of a different price: if the parties agree to a higher or lower amount, the condition is void but the iqaalah itself remains valid at the original price. This is based on the same principle that iqaalah is a rescission of the original contract, not a new contract with its own price.

The third ruling presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Hanifa, iqaalah is a rescission (faskh) for the two contracting parties, but it is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) vis-à-vis third parties. This has a practical consequence: third parties, such as a pre-emption (shuf'a) claimant, can assert their rights because they see the transfer of property as a new sale. The opposing view (of the Sahibayn, though not fully detailed here) would treat it as faskh for all purposes.

The fourth and fifth rulings deal with destruction of the subject matter. If the price (thaman) is destroyed, iqaalah is still valid because the price can be replaced with other identical goods; but if the specific sold item (mabee') is destroyed, iqaalah is invalid because the specific item cannot be returned. If only part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remaining part, with a proportional refund of the price.

This passage is a typical sharh that expands on a short matn, providing proofs (hadith and athar) and clarifying conditions. The legal reasoning is rooted in the nature of iqaalah as faskh versus a new sale, and the distinction between fungible and specific property.

Key Terms
اقالة (iqaalah): The mutual rescission of a sale contract by both parties, returning the sold item and price.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid, which must be returned in exact amount in iqaalah.
مبيع (mabee'): The sold item that must be returned; if destroyed, iqaalah invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in the passage, what is the ruling if the parties stipulate a price higher than the original in iqaalah?
A) The iqaalah is invalid and the sale remains.
B) The condition is void, but iqaalah is valid at the original price.
C) The iqaalah is valid at the higher price.
D) The iqaalah is suspended until a fair price is determined.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah is permissible only at the original price; any stipulation of a different price voids the condition but not the rescission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Legal Nature of Iqaalah: Faskh vs New Sale and Effect of Destruction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on legal nature and rules on destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's view on iqaalah as faskh for parties and new sale for third parties, and apply the rules regarding destruction of price vs sold item.

Core Idea
Iqaalah is faskh for the parties but a new sale for third parties according to Abu Hanifa; destruction of the sold item prevents iqaalah, destruction of price does not.

This lesson delves into the legal nature of iqaalah. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, iqaalah is a rescission (faskh) for the contracting parties, but it appears as a new sale to third parties, thereby permitting claims of pre-emption (shuf'a). The passage also addresses practical scenarios: if the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah cannot take place because the specific item cannot be returned; but if the price is destroyed, iqaalah remains valid because the price can be replaced. We will study the proofs and the practical differences these rulings create.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on legal nature
According to Abu Hanifa, iqaalah is faskh for the two contracting parties but a new sale for others. Understand why: the contract is undone between them, but third parties see a transfer of property.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; Sahibayn say it is faskh for all.
2
Analyze the third-party effect
Why does this matter? The right of shuf'a (pre-emption) is established because third parties see it as a new sale. If it were purely faskh, shuf'a would not arise.
Practical application: a neighbor can claim shuf'a when property is returned via iqaalah.
3
Examine destruction of price (thaman)
If the price is destroyed, iqaalah is still valid. Why? Price is fungible (mithli), so another identical amount can be given.
The passage says: 'Destruction of the price does not prevent validity.'
4
Examine destruction of sold item (mabee')
If the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is invalid. The item is specific ('ayn); it cannot be returned.
Iqaalah requires return of the exact item; if destroyed, impossible.
5
Apply partial destruction
If part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remaining part with proportional refund.
Example: 6 kg wheat bought for 10 Rs; 3 kg destroyed; return remaining 3 kg and take 5 Rs.

[۹۱۰) (۱) اقالہ جائز ہے بیچ میں بائع کے لئے اور مشتری کے لئے شمن اول کے مثل ہے۔ بائع اور مشتری دونوں کے لئے اقالہ جائز ہے۔ لیکن جس قیمت میں بیچ ہوئی تھی بائع اتنی ہی قیمت واپس کر دیگا، کم بھی نہیں اور زیادہ بھی نہیں۔ بائع نے جتنی قیمت پہلے لی ہے وہی قیمت واپس کرے، اسی کو بمثل الثمن الاول کہا ہے۔ (۱) بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان کوئی نئی بیع نہیں ہے کہ قیمت زیادہ لے یا کم لے بلکہ اقالہ کا مطلب ہے۔ پہلے ہی بیع کو توڑنا ہے اس لئے پہلی ہی قیمت واپس کرے گا (۲) عن ابن عباس انه كره ان يبتاع البيع ثم يرده ويرد معه دراهم وفي هذا دلالة على ان الاقالة فسخ فلا تجوز الابرأس المال (ب) ( سنن سمجھتی ، باب من اقال المسلم اليه بعض المسلم و قبض بعضاج سادس ص ۴۵، نمبر ۱۱۱۳۳) عن الاسود انه كره ان يردها و يرد معها شيئا (ج) مصنف عبد الرزاق ج ثامن ص ۱۹ نمبر (۱۴۱۳) اس اثر میں حضرت عبداللہ بن عباس نے شمن کے ساتھ مزید کچھ دینے سے کراہیت کا اظہار کیا ہے۔
اقالہ بیع اول کا فسخ ہے۔
،

او پر ابن عباس کا اثر گزر چکا ہے کہ زیادہ دینا وہ مکروہ سمجھتے تھے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے فرمایا کسی نے مسلمان سے اقالہ کیا تو اللہ اس کے گناہوں کو معاف کر دے گا (ب) حضرت عبد اللہ بن عباس نا پسند کرتے تھے کہ آدمی بیچ کرے پھر اس کو واپس لوٹائے اور اس کے ساتھ کچھ درہم لوٹائے۔ اس قول میں اس بات پر دلالت ہے کہ اقالہ کرنا بیچ کو فتح کرتا ہے۔ اس لئے نہیں جائز ہے مگر راس المال یعنی پہلی قیمت کے ساتھ (ج) حضرت اسود نا پسند فرماتے تھے کہ مبیع واپس کرے اور اس کے ساتھ کچھ اور بھی واپس کرے۔

اصول اقالہ میں اصل واپسی مبیع کی ہوتی ہے۔ (۱۹۱۴ (۵) اگر بعض مبیع ہلاک ہو جائے تو باقی میں اقالہ جائز ہے۔

Chapter of Iqaalah. Book of Sales. [10] (1) Iqaalah is permissible in sale for the seller and the buyer at the like of the first price. [911] (2) Then if he stipulates more than it, the condition is void and he returns at the like of the first price. The meaning of iqaalah: the seller regrets after selling, asks the buyer to return the sold item and take back the price, and the buyer does so; or the buyer regrets and asks the seller to take back the sold item and give the price. Doing so is permissible, rather preferable. Evidence from hadith: From Abu Huraira, he said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever rescinds a sale for a Muslim, Allah will forgive his slips.' (Abu Dawood, Ibn Majah). This hadith indicates iqaalah is permissible and rewardable. [910] (1) Iqaalah is permissible in sale for the seller and the buyer at the like of the first price. The seller returns exactly the price he took. (2) From Ibn Abbas: he disliked that a man sell then return with extra dirhams. This indicates iqaalah is faskh (rescission), so it is not permissible except at the original capital. From Aswad: he disliked returning with extra. Iqaalah is faskh of the first sale. [911] (2) Then if a condition of more or less than the first price is stipulated, the condition is void, and the first price is returned. Example: bought for four hundred, buyer demands five hundred or seller offers three hundred, the condition is void but iqaalah is valid at the first price. Footnote: (a) He said: whoever rescinds for a Muslim, Allah will forgive his sins. (b) Ibn Abbas disliked returning with extra dirhams; this indicates iqaalah is faskh, so not valid except at original capital. (c) Aswad disliked returning with extra. Then: if he stipulates more than it, the condition is void and he returns at the like of the first price. [12] (3) It is faskh for the two contracting parties, a new sale for others, according to Abu Hanifa. [913] (4) Destruction of the price does not prevent validity of iqaalah, and destruction of the sold item prevents its validity. (1914) (5) And if some of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remainder. Commentary: (3) Iqaalah is faskh for the two parties and a new sale for others in the view of Imam Abu Hanifa. The third party sees the sold item moving from buyer to seller with offer and acceptance, so it is like a new sale. Therefore, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) can be claimed. (4) Destruction of price does not prevent iqaalah because price is fungible; destruction of sold item prevents because the item is specific. (5) If part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remaining portion. Example: six kilos of wheat bought for ten rupees; three kilos destroyed; remaining three can be returned for five rupees. Footnote: (a) Ibn Abbas disliked returning with extra; this indicates iqaalah is faskh, not valid except at original capital.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on Iqaalah (rescission of sale) in Kitab al-Buyoo'. The author begins by defining iqaalah: it is when either the seller or the buyer regrets the transaction and the other party agrees to annul it. The first ruling states that iqaalah is permissible (ja'iz) and even preferable (afdal), provided it is done at the original price (bi-mithl al-thaman al-awwal). The proof is a hadith: 'Whoever rescinds for a Muslim, Allah will rescind his sins.' This is a strong encouragement. Then an athar from Ibn Abbas shows that returning the sold item with extra money is disliked, indicating that iqaalah is a rescission (faskh), not a new sale, so no extra payment is allowed.

The second ruling addresses a stipulation of a different price: if the parties agree to a higher or lower amount, the condition is void but the iqaalah itself remains valid at the original price. This is based on the same principle that iqaalah is a rescission of the original contract, not a new contract with its own price.

The third ruling presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute. According to Abu Hanifa, iqaalah is a rescission (faskh) for the two contracting parties, but it is considered a new sale (bay' jadid) vis-à-vis third parties. This has a practical consequence: third parties, such as a pre-emption (shuf'a) claimant, can assert their rights because they see the transfer of property as a new sale. The opposing view (of the Sahibayn, though not fully detailed here) would treat it as faskh for all purposes.

The fourth and fifth rulings deal with destruction of the subject matter. If the price (thaman) is destroyed, iqaalah is still valid because the price can be replaced with other identical goods; but if the specific sold item (mabee') is destroyed, iqaalah is invalid because the specific item cannot be returned. If only part of the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the remaining part, with a proportional refund of the price.

This passage is a typical sharh that expands on a short matn, providing proofs (hadith and athar) and clarifying conditions. The legal reasoning is rooted in the nature of iqaalah as faskh versus a new sale, and the distinction between fungible and specific property.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of a contract, ab initio, as if it never existed.
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption right: a neighboring property owner's right to purchase a sold property before a stranger.
هلاك (halak): Destruction; if the sold item is destroyed, iqaalah is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of the destruction of the price (thaman) on iqaalah?
A) Iqaalah becomes invalid.
B) Iqaalah is still valid.
C) Iqaalah is valid only if the seller forgives the price.
D) Iqaalah is suspended until a new price is paid.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah is faskh for the parties but a new sale for third parties; destruction of the sold item invalidates iqaalah, but destruction of the price does not.

The Price Condition and Legal Nature of Iqalah
Ruling with proof on price condition and nature of Iqalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the condition of price in Iqalah and explain the legal nature of Iqalah as a rescission between the parties and a new sale for third parties.

Core Idea
Iqalah is a rescission between the contracting parties; any condition for extra payment is void.

Iqalah (mutual rescission) is a common transaction in which buyer and seller agree to unwind a sale. The Hanafi school has specific rules about what can be returned and the legal status of the transaction. This lesson examines the condition that the buyer returns exactly the first price—no more—and explores the nature of Iqalah: is it a rescission or a new sale? We will see how the answer affects third parties like preemptors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling on Price Condition
Read the first part of the passage: 'If he stipulates more than it, the condition is void, and he returns the like of the first price.' Note that any condition for extra payment is null; the return must be the exact original price.
2
Determine the Legal Nature of Iqalah (Abu Hanifa's View)
The passage states: 'And it is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties, a new sale with respect to others.' This is Abu Hanifa's position. Understand that for the original parties, the contract is annulled; for third parties (e.g., a preemptor), it is treated as a fresh sale.
3
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The passage cites the athar of Ibn Abbas: he disapproved of a person buying and then returning with extra dirhams. This indicates that Iqalah must be a rescission, not a new sale with profit.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Bayhaqi; it is zanni in transmission and meaning.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that Iqalah is a new sale for all parties, including the original ones. Identify their position and its basis (the exchange of goods and price with new offer and acceptance).
5
Evaluate the Practical Consequence (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, a preemptor cannot claim shuf'ah after Iqalah because it is a new sale; under the Sahibayn's view, the preemptor could still claim. Similarly, options (khiyar) are affected. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرط اکثر منه فالشرط باطل ويرد بمثل الثمن الاول. وهي فسخ في حق المتعاقدين بيع جديد في حق غيرهما في قول ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. عن ابن عباس انه كره ان يبتاع البيع ثم برده و يردمعه دراهم وفي هذا دلالة على ان الاقالة فسخ فلا تجوز الا برأس المال (سن للبيھقی، باب من اقال المسلم اليه بعض المسلم وقبض بعضا [؟] ج سادس ص ٤٥ نمبر ١١٣)
[If] he stipulates more than it, the condition is void and he returns the like of the first price. And it (Iqalah) is a rescission with respect to the two contracting parties, a new sale with respect to others in the saying of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. From Ibn Abbas that he disapproved that a person buys a sale then returns it and returns with it dirhams, and in this is evidence that Iqalah is a rescission, so it is not permissible except with the capital.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage addresses two foundational rules of Iqalah (mutual rescission of a sale). The first rule: if the parties agree on a condition that the buyer returns more than the original price, that condition is void and the return must be exactly the same price. This is because Iqalah is a rescission, not a new sale; it restores the previous state. The second rule concerns the nature of Iqalah according to Imam Abu Hanifa: between the two original parties, it is a rescission (faskh), meaning the original contract is annulled. However, for third parties (e.g., a preemptor or a subsequent buyer), it is considered a new sale. This distinction matters for rights like preemption (shuf'ah): a person claiming preemption cannot do so based on the original sale after Iqalah, because for him it is a new sale. The proof from Ibn Abbas shows disapproval of returning with additional dirhams, indicating Iqalah must be with the original capital only. Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ: they consider Iqalah a new sale for all parties. But the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the basis for many practical rulings.

Key Terms
إقالة (Iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale by both parties, returning the commodity and price.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the original contract ab initio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the legal nature of Iqalah (mutual rescission) for the two original parties?
A) It is a new sale for all parties.
B) It is a rescission (faskh) for the parties and a new sale for third parties.
C) It is a pure gift (hibah) without consideration.
D) It is invalid if the price has changed.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah is a rescission between the parties; any condition for extra payment is void, and the legal nature affects preemption and other third-party rights.

Effect of Destruction of Price or Commodity on Iqalah
Stating that destruction of price does not prevent Iqalah, but destruction of commodity does

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of destruction of the price (thaman) and the commodity (mabi') on the validity of Iqalah, and apply this ruling to scenarios of partial destruction and defects.

Core Idea
Destruction of the commodity prevents Iqalah; destruction of the price does not — because the commodity is specific, the price is fungible.

Iqalah (mutual rescission of sale) requires returning the subject matter. In this passage, we learn how the destruction of either the price or the commodity affects the validity of Iqalah. The key lies in whether the item is specific and identifiable or fungible and replaceable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements
Recognize the price (thaman) and the commodity (mabi') in the transaction. Understand that thaman is fungible (replaceable by similar) while mabi' is specific and unique.
2
Apply the rule to destruction scenarios
Apply the ruling: if thaman is destroyed, Iqalah remains valid because it can be replaced with a similar price. If mabi' is destroyed, Iqalah is invalid because the specific commodity no longer exists to be returned.
3
Consider partial destruction and defects
If part of the mabi' is destroyed, Iqalah is valid for the remaining portion with proportional price return. If the mabi' has a defect (not destroyed), the buyer may return it with a reduced price.
This shows the flexibility of Iqalah when the commodity is not completely gone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهلاك الثمن لا يمنع صحة الاقالة وهلاك المبيع يمنع صحتها. وان شمن پر اس وقت اقالہ ہوگا جبکہ مبیع میں کوئی عیب نہ ہوا ہو۔ اگر مبیع میں عیب ہو گیا تو مشتری کم قیمت واپس کر سکتا ہے۔ هلك بعض المبيع جازت الاقالة في باقيه. اصول اقالہ میں اصل واپسی مبیع کی ہوتی ہے۔
And the destruction of the price does not prevent the validity of Iqalah, and the destruction of the sold commodity prevents its validity. And Iqalah will be at that time when no defect has occurred in the sold commodity; if a defect occurred in the sold commodity, the buyer can return [it] with a reduced price. If part of the sold commodity is destroyed, Iqalah is permissible in the remainder. The principle in Iqalah is that the return is primarily of the sold commodity.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage explains the effect of destruction on Iqalah. The core principle is that the return is primarily of the sold commodity (mabi'), which is specific and identifiable. Therefore, if the mabi' is completely destroyed, Iqalah is invalid because there is nothing to return. However, the price (thaman) is fungible (mithli), so its destruction does not prevent Iqalah; the buyer can return a similar price. If the mabi' has a defect occurred after the sale, Iqalah is still valid, but the buyer may return it with a reduction in the price proportionate to the defect (since the defect reduces value). If only part of the mabi' is destroyed, Iqalah is valid for the remaining part, and the price is returned proportionally. For example, if 3 of 6 measures of wheat are destroyed, the buyer can return the remaining 3 and recover half the price. The condition that 'no defect has occurred' in the text seems to refer to the initial validity; if a defect occurs, the buyer has the option to return at a reduced price, which is a form of modified Iqalah. The Hanafi madhhab emphasizes the specificity of the commodity as the reason for these rules.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price (money or fungible) paid in the sale.
مبيع (mabi'): The sold commodity, which is specific and identified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the sold commodity (mabi') is completely destroyed before Iqalah, what is the ruling?
A) Iqalah is still valid because the price can be returned.
B) Iqalah is not valid because there is nothing to return.
C) Iqalah is valid only if the destruction was not due to the buyer's negligence.
D) Iqalah becomes a new sale for the destroyed portion.

Key Takeaway
Destruction of the sold commodity (mabi') prevents Iqalah because the return is of a specific item; destruction of the fungible price (thaman) does not prevent Iqalah.

Validity of Iqaalah When Part of Sold Item Is Destroyed
Ruling on iqaalah when part of the sold item perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqaalah when part of the sold item perishes and apply it to scenarios involving multiple commodities.

Core Idea
Iqaalah is valid for the remaining part even if some of the sold item is destroyed.

This lesson presents a key Hanafi ruling in sales (buyu'): if part of the sold item is destroyed, the mutual rescission (iqaalah) can still be valid for the remaining part. The passage also illustrates a scenario where both parties had goods that could serve as the subject matter, further confirming the flexibility of iqaalah. This ruling highlights the Hanafi view that iqaalah is a separate contract of rescission, not dependent on the full existence of the original sold item.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the ruling
State the core rule: 'هلك بعض المبيع جازت الاقالة في باقيه' – iqaalah is valid for the remainder.
2
Understand the scenario
Consider a case where both sides had goods that could be sold (e.g., wheat and mill contents). If the wheat perishes, iqaalah is still valid for the remaining goods because they are specific and can be sold.
The remaining goods must be specific and capable of being the subject of a sale.
3
Apply the ruling
Apply the rule to a similar situation: if a buyer purchases a multi-item lot and some items are destroyed, iqaalah is valid for the undestroyed items.
The ruling does not require the entire original mabīʿ to exist for iqaalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هلك بعض المبيع جازت الاقالة في باقيه.
و اگر دونوں طرف سامان ہی تھا مثلا مبیع گیہوں اور مشن میں جو تھا تو چونکہ دونوں مبیع بن سکتے ہیں۔ اور دونوں متعین ہوتے ہیں اس لئے اگر مبیع مثلا گیہوں ہلاک ہو گیا تو جو بیع بن سکتا ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں بھی اقالہ صحیح ہو سکے گا۔
If part of the sold item perishes, iqaalah (rescission) is valid for the remainder. And if both sides were the commodity, e.g., the sold item was wheat and what was in the mill, since both can become sold items and both are specific, therefore if the sold item, e.g., wheat, perishes, then because it can be sold, in this case too iqaalah is valid.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: if some of the sold item is destroyed, the iqaalah (mutual rescission of the sale) remains valid for the remaining part. The author then explains a specific scenario: if both parties had goods that could serve as the sold item (e.g., wheat and mill contents), and the wheat perishes, since the other item can still be sold, iqaalah is still valid. This is a Hanafi ruling that emphasizes the permissibility of iqaalah even when part of the subject matter is lost.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqaalah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract by agreement of both parties.
مبيع (mabīʿ): The item being sold; the subject matter of the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Iqaalah becomes invalid entirely.
B) Iqaalah is valid for the remaining part.
C) Iqaalah requires a new contract.
D) Iqaalah is only valid if both parties are present.

Key Takeaway
Iqaalah remains valid for the remaining part of the sold item even after partial destruction.

Murabahah and Tawliyah: Definitions and Proofs
Defines murabahah and tawliyah with textual proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define murabahah and tawliyah, state their essential condition of price disclosure, and cite the Hanafi proofs from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
In trust-based sales, disclosing the original cost is mandatory — without it, the sale is ordinary, not murabahah or tawliyah.

This passage introduces two special types of sale that rely on the seller's honesty: murabahah (sale with disclosed profit) and tawliyah (sale at cost). Both require the seller to reveal the original purchase price. Understanding these definitions is essential for distinguishing trust-based transactions from ordinary sales.

Guided Path
1
Define murabahah
From the text: transfer of ownership at the first price plus an increase of profit (ribḥ). The profit must be known and agreed upon.
The word 'murabahah' is derived from ribḥ (profit).
2
Define tawliyah
From the text: transfer of ownership at the first price without any increase of profit. The seller makes the buyer a trustee (wali) at cost.
Tawliyah comes from wilayah (guardianship/trust).
3
Identify the essential condition
Both sales require disclosure of the original purchase price. If the seller does not reveal the cost, the transaction is an ordinary sale, not murabahah or tawliyah.
This condition prevents deception (gharar) and builds trust.
4
Examine the proof for murabahah
The athar of 'Ali (RA): He bought a garment for five dirhams and offered to sell it for six dirhams (one dirham profit), disclosing the cost. This shows the practice was known among the Companions.
Reference: Sunan al-Bayhaqi no. 10794.
5
Examine the proof for tawliyah
The hadith of the Prophet (PBUH) with Abu Bakr (RA): The Prophet said 'at the price' (bil-thaman) meaning the same price Abu Bakr had paid. This indicates selling at cost with disclosure.
Reference: Sahih al-Bukhari no. 3905.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المرابحة والتولية
[۱۵] (۱) المرابحة نقل ما ملكه بالعقد الاول بالثمن الاول مع زيادة ربح [۱۶] (۲) والتولية نقل ما ملكه بالعقد الاول بالثمن الاول من غير زيادة ربح.

ضروری نوٹ مرابحہ : کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جتنے میں خریدا بائع مشتری کو صاف بتائے کہ میں نے مثلا دس پونڈ میں یہ بیع خریدی ہے اور دو پوند نفع لیکر بارہ پونڈ میں آپ کے ہاتھ بیچتا ہوں۔ اس میں دو پونڈ نفع لیا اس لئے اس کو مرابحہ کہتے ہیں۔ اگر بائع نفع لے لیکن مشتری کو یہ نہ بتائے کہ کتنے میں خریدا ہے تو یہ عام بیچ ہے۔ اس کو مرابحہ نہیں کہیں گے۔ مرابحہ میں پہلی قیمت بتانا ضروری ہے۔ یہ اس لئے ہوتا ہے تا کہ مشتری کو اعتماد ہو اور دھوکہ نہ ہو۔ اس کا ثبوت اس اثر میں ہے۔ رایت علی علی آزارا غليظا قال اشتريت بخمسة دراهم فمن ار بحني فيه درهما بعته اياه (سن سمجھتی ، باب المرابحه ج خاص ص ۵۳۸ نمبر ۱۰۷۹۴) اس اثر میں پانچ درہم میں از ارخریدی تھی اور ایک درہم مرابحہ پر حضرت علی بیچنا چاہتے تھے۔ جس سے بیع مرابحہ کا ثبوت ہوا۔ تولیہ کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بائع مشتری کو بتائے کہ میں نے مثلا دس پونڈ میں یہ مبیع خریدی ہے اور دس ہی پونڈ میں بیچتا ہوں۔ جتنے میں خریدی اتنے ہی میں مبیع کا ولی بنادینے کو تولیہ کہتے ہیں۔ اگر نہیں بتایا کہ کتنے میں خریدی تو یہ تولیہ نہیں ہے، عام بیع ہے۔ اس بیج کا ثبوت اس حدیث میں ہے قالت عائشة فبينما نحن يوما جلوس في بيت ابي بكر ... قال ابو بكر فخذ بابی انت یا رسول الله احدى راحلتي هاتين قال رسول الله بالثمن (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب ہجرة النبي و اصحابہ الی المدینی ص ۵۵۳ نمبر ۳۹۰۵ / بخاری شریف ، باب اذا اشتری متاعا او دابتہ فوضعه عند البائع ص ۲۸۷ نمبر ۲۱۳۸) اس حدیث میں حضور نے ابو بکر سے فرمایا کہ جتنے میں اونٹنی خریدی ہے اتنے ہی میں دیدے۔ اس لئے آپ نے فرمایا بالثمن، یعنی بالثمن الاول ،اس لئے اس سے بیع تولیہ کا ثبوت ہوا۔
:
[۹۱۵) (۱) بیع مرابحہ منتقل کرنا ہے جس چیز کا مالک بنا عقد اول میں شمن اول سے نفع کی زیادتی کے ساتھ ۔
ان پہلی بیچ میں جس بیع کا جتنی قیمت سے مالک بنا ہے اسی قیمت پر کچھ نفع لیکر بیچنے کو مرابحہ کہتے ہیں۔ مرابحہ ریح سے مشتق ہے۔ جس کے معنی نفع لینا ہے۔ اس لئے پہلی قیمت پر نفع لے گا۔ اس لئے اس کو مرابحہ کہتے ہیں۔ [۹۱۶) (۲) اور بیع تولیہ وہ منتقل کرنا ہے جس کا مالک بنا عقد اول سے ثمن اول کے ساتھ بغیر نفع کی زیادتی کے۔
جتنے میں پہلی بیع میں خریدا ہے اتنی قیمت میں بیچ دینے کو تو لیہ کہتے ہیں۔ چونکہ پہلی ہی قیمت میں مشتری کو طبیع کا ولی بنانا ہے اور اس پر کچھ نفع نہیں لینا ہے اس لئے اس بیج کو تولیہ کہتے ہیں (۲) مرابحہ اور تولیہ دونوں بیوع کی دلیل ضروری نوٹ میں گزرگئی ( بخاری شریف نمبر ۳۹۰۵ رسنن بیہقی، نمبر ۱۰۷۹۴)
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابو بکر نے فرمایا میرے ماں باپ آپ پر فدا ہوں میری ان دو اونٹنیوں میں سے ایک آپ لے لیں۔ آپ نے فرمایا پہلی قیمت سے لوں گا۔
Chapter on Murabahah and Tawliyah [15] (1) Murabahah is the transfer of what one owned by the first contract at the first price with an increase of profit. [16] (2) And Tawliyah is the transfer of what one owned by the first contract at the first price without any increase of profit. ... Necessary note: The meaning of murabahah is that the seller clearly tells the buyer that he bought this item, for example, for ten pounds, and he sells it for twelve pounds taking two pounds profit. Because he took two pounds profit, it is called murabahah. If the seller takes profit but does not inform the buyer of the purchase price, it is an ordinary sale, not called murabahah. In murabahah, disclosing the first price is necessary. This is done so that the buyer has confidence and there is no deception. Its proof is in this athar: 'I saw 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) with a thick garment; he said: I bought it for five dirhams, so whoever wants me to profit one dirham in it, I will sell it to him.' (Sunan al-Kubra, Bab al-Murabahah, specific, p. 538, no. 10794). In this athar, he had bought it for five dirhams and wanted to sell it on murabahah with one dirham profit. This proves the murabahah sale. The meaning of tawliyah is that the seller tells the buyer: I bought this item, for example, for ten pounds, and I sell it for ten pounds. Handing over the sold item at the same price as purchased is called tawliyah. If he does not disclose the purchase price, it is not tawliyah, but an ordinary sale. The proof of this sale is in the hadith: 'A'ishah said: While we were sitting one day in Abu Bakr's house... Abu Bakr said: Take, O Messenger of Allah, one of my two she-camels. The Messenger of Allah said: At the price.' (Bukhari, Bab Hijrat al-Nabi wa Ashabihi ila al-Madinah, p. 553, no. 3905; Bukhari, Bab idha ishtara mata'an aw dabbatan fawada'ahu 'inda al-ba'i', p. 287, no. 2138). In this hadith, the Prophet told Abu Bakr to give it at the same price he bought the she-camel. So he said 'at the price', meaning the first price, thus proving the tawliyah sale. [915] (1) Bai' Murabahah is transferring ownership of something which became owned by the first contract, with the first price plus an increase of profit. The first sale in which one became owner at a certain price, selling it at that price with some profit is called murabahah. Murabahah is derived from ribh, meaning profit. So he takes profit on the first price, hence it is called murabahah. [916] (2) And Bai' Tawliyah is transferring ownership which became owned by the first contract with the first price without any increase of profit. Selling at the same price at which it was purchased in the first sale is called tawliyah. Since the buyer is made the wali (custodian) of the commodity at the first price and no profit is taken, this sale is called tawliyah. (2) The evidence for both murabahah and tawliyah has passed in the necessary note (Bukhari no. 3905, Sunan al-Bayhaqi, no. 10794). Marginal note: (a) Abu Bakr said: May my parents be sacrificed for you, take one of these two she-camels. The Prophet said: I will take it at the [first] price.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines two types of trust-based sales: murabahah (sale at cost plus disclosed profit) and tawliyah (sale at cost without profit). The author emphasizes that disclosure of the original purchase price is essential for both to be valid; otherwise, the transaction is considered an ordinary sale. The proofs provided are from the actions of 'Ali (RA) for murabahah and from the hadith of the Prophet (PBUH) with Abu Bakr (RA) for tawliyah. The commentary explains that these sales are based on honesty and transparency to prevent deception. The Hanafi school accepts these contracts as valid forms of sale.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the original purchase price and sells at that price plus a stated profit.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership at the same price he purchased, without any profit.
ربح (ribḥ): Profit; the additional amount over the cost price in a murabahah sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the essential condition for a sale to be considered murabahah or tawliyah according to the passage?
A) The seller must make a profit.
B) The seller must disclose the original purchase price.
C) The sale must be in cash.
D) The buyer must be a relative.

Key Takeaway
Disclosure of the original purchase price is the defining condition for both murabahah and tawliyah in Hanafi fiqh.

The Mithli Price Requirement for Murabahah and Tawliyah
States that murabahah and tawliyah require the price to be mithli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of mithli for murabahah and tawliyah, and apply it to identify which goods are permitted for these sales.

Core Idea
Murabahah and tawliyah are invalid unless the original price is mithli—exactly matchable.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational condition for two important types of sale: murabahah (cost-plus) and tawliyah (sale at cost). The Hanafi school requires that the original price (thaman) must be a mithli commodity—one that can be exactly duplicated. This condition avoids uncertainty (gharar) and ensures transparency. Let's explore why and how this works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Murabahah and tawliyah are sales that require disclosure of cost. The passage states they are not valid unless the price is mithli.
2
Define mithli
Mithli refers to a commodity that can be exactly matched in quantity and quality, such as grains, oils, money, and other homogeneous goods.
3
Explain the rationale
The buyer needs to know the exact cost to calculate profit or accept the price. Non-mithli items (e.g., unique animals, real estate) cannot be exactly matched, leading to uncertainty (gharar) which invalidates the sale.
4
Provide examples
Valid: selling wheat at cost plus profit; Invalid: selling a specific cow at cost plus profit because cows are not identical.
5
Contrast with non-mithli
Items like livestock, unique antiques, or real estate are not mithli; therefore, murabahah and tawliyah on them are invalid in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تصح المرابحة والتولية حتى يكون العوض مما له مثل
And murabahah and tawliyah are not valid until the consideration is of a type that has an equivalent.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This is a ruling from the matn. The commentator explains that murabahah (cost-plus sale) and tawliyah (resale at cost) are only valid when the original price (thaman) is mithli – that is, a commodity that can be exactly matched in quantity and quality, like wheat, rice, dirhams and dinars. Items like cows or buffaloes are not mithli because exact equivalents are not available. The rationale is that in these transactions, the buyer needs to know the exact cost to calculate profit or take at cost. If the price is not mithli, there is uncertainty, so the transaction is invalid.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin.
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller resells the item at exactly the same price he paid, without profit.
مثلي (mithli): A commodity that can be exactly matched in quantity and quality, like grains, money, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is murabahah invalid if the original price is not mithli?
A) Because the seller would be lying about the cost.
B) Because there is uncertainty (gharar) in matching the exact price.
C) Because the buyer cannot know the profit margin.
D) Because it is considered riba.

Key Takeaway
Murabahah and tawliyah are valid only when the original price is a mithli commodity; non-mithli prices introduce gharar and invalidate the sale.

What Costs Can Be Added to Capital in Murabahah?
Lists wage and transport costs that can be added to capital in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expenses may be added to the capital in a murabahah sale and explain the underlying condition of value enhancement.

Core Idea
Only expenses that increase the item’s value may be added to the capital in murabahah.

In a murabahah sale, the seller must disclose the cost price and the profit margin. But which costs can be included in that original cost? This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling: only expenses that directly enhance the item’s value—like laundering, dyeing, or transport to a higher-value market—may be added. Other costs, such as storage or personal expenses, cannot be included.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the Murabahah Framework
Recall that murabahah is a cost-plus sale: the seller must truthfully disclose the original cost. This step sets the stage knowing what is at stake.
2
List the Permissible Additions from the Text
Read the Arabic passage and identify each listed item: launderer (al-qassar), dyer (al-sabbagh), embroiderer (al-tiraz), twister (al-fatl), and transport (haml al-ta‘am).
These are all services that transform the item.
3
Identify the Common Condition
What do all these expenses have in common? They each add value: laundering cleans and presses, dyeing colours, embroidery decorates, twisting strengthens, transport moves goods to a more valuable market.
The key condition is that the expense must enhance the item’s value.
4
Distinguish from Impermissible Additions
Expenses that do not increase value—such as storage, insurance, or the seller’s personal travel—cannot be added. The buyer must not be charged for costs that do not improve the product.
Adding such costs would make the disclosed capital inaccurate and the sale potentially fasid.
5
Apply to Modern Scenarios
Think of modern equivalents: photography for an online listing, professional grading of a collectible, or certification fees. Do these add value? If yes, they can be included; if merely incidental, they cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجوز ان يضيف الى رأس المال اجرة القصار والصباغ والطراز والفتل واجرة حمل الطعام
And it is permissible to add to the capital the wage of the launderer, the dyer, the embroiderer, the twister, and the cost of transporting food.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This ruling allows the seller in a murabahah transaction to include certain expenses in the disclosed capital cost. The listed expenses are those that enhance the value of the item: laundering increases the cloth's sheen, dyeing adds color, embroidery and twisting add workmanship, and transport costs can increase the price when goods are moved to a market where they are more valuable. The commentator cites an athar (tradition) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that permits adding these costs. The seller must say 'it cost me such-and-such' rather than 'I bought it for such-and-such' to avoid lying (as per the next ruling). The footnote provides the hadith source from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.

Key Terms
القصار (al-qassar): The launderer or washerman who cleans and presses cloth.
الصباغ (al-sabbagh): The dyer who colors the cloth.
الطراز (al-tiraz): The embroiderer or one who decorates cloth.
الفتل (al-fatl): The twister (e.g., of ropes or threads).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Storage cost without value increase
C) Personal travel expenses
D) Advertising cost

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi murabahah, only value-enhancing costs can be added to the disclosed capital; inclusion of unrelated expenses violates the contract's transparency.

Proper Wording in Murabahah: State Total Cost
Instructs seller to say 'it cost me' rather than 'I bought it for'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required wording in murabahah when total cost exceeds purchase price and explain why the alternative is prohibited.

Core Idea
In murabahah, say 'it cost me' not 'I bought it for' to avoid falsehood.

Murabahah is a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit. Often the seller incurs additional permissible expenses beyond the purchase price. Using the phrase 'I bought it for' would be false in such cases. Hanafi fiqh therefore instructs the seller to say 'it cost me' (qaama 'alayya) to truthfully reflect the total outlay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states the seller must say 'it cost me' and must not say 'I bought it for'. Recognize this as a directive to ensure truthfulness.
2
Understand the reason
Permissible expenses added to the purchase price increase the total cost. Saying 'I bought it for' would understate the cost and constitute a lie.
Lying is haram.
3
Compare the two wordings
'I bought it for' refers only to the purchase price; 'it cost me' includes all permissible additions. The latter is accurate because the seller's actual expenditure is higher.
4
Apply the ruling
In a scenario, practice stating the correct phrase. For example, if an item was bought for $100 and $10 shipping was added, the seller says 'it cost me $110'.
This maintains trust.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقول قام على بكذا ولا يقول اشتريته بكذا
And he should say 'it cost me such-and-such' and should not say 'I bought it for such-and-such'.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
When a seller in murabahah has added permissible expenses to the capital, he must not say 'I bought it for such-and-such' because that would be a lie – he paid more than the purchase price after adding costs. Instead, he should say 'it cost me such-and-such' (qaama 'alayya bi-kadha) to be truthful. This ruling emphasizes honesty in transactions. The commentator adds that a person should always speak the truth to maintain trust.

Key Terms
قام على (qaama 'ala): A phrase meaning 'it cost me' or 'it stood at' – used to indicate total cost including additions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the buyer prefers that wording.
D) Because it is a Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, the seller must truthfully disclose the total cost using 'it cost me' to avoid falsehood.

Buyer's Option When Fraud Discovered in Murabahah
Gives buyer the option to take or return item if seller commits fraud in murabahah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's options upon discovering fraud in a murabahah contract according to Abu Hanifa, and distinguish between taking the item at the stated price and returning it.

Core Idea
Fraud in murabahah gives the buyer a choice: accept the inflated price or rescind.

In murabahah sales, the seller must honestly disclose the cost price. If he lies about it, the buyer discovers the fraud, and has a legal option (khiyar) under Abu Hanifa's ruling. This lesson unpacks that option and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Murabahah Contract
Recall that murabahah is a sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds an agreed profit.
Fraud occurs when the seller misstates the cost.
2
Identify the Fraud
The buyer discovers the seller lied about the cost (e.g., said 12 when actual cost was 10).
This is khiyanah (treachery).
3
Apply the Ruling of Khiyar
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to either accept the item at the stated price or return it.
He cannot force a price correction.
4
Determine Consequences
If he keeps the item, he pays the full stated price; if he returns, the contract is rescinded.
The contract itself is sahih (valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان اطلع المشترى على خيانة فى المرابحة فهو بالخيار عند ابي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى ان شاء اخذه بجميع الثمن وان شاء رده
Then if the buyer discovers fraud in the murabahah, he has the option, according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, to take it at the full price or to return it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
If the seller lies about the cost in a murabahah transaction (e.g., he says the cost was 12 when it was 10, thereby taking extra profit), the buyer, upon discovering this, has the option (khiyar) to either accept the item at the full stated price (including the inflated profit) or to return it. The buyer cannot force the seller to reduce the price to the correct profit margin because the contract was made with the term 'murabahah' which implies a specific profit. The commentator explains that the contract is valid as murabahah, but the lie gives the buyer the right of rescission. The text includes a hadith from Tirmidhi warning against false oaths in selling. The footnote gives the hadith text: three types of people Allah will not look at on the Day of Judgment, including the one who sells his goods by false oath.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): An option or right to choose between upholding or canceling a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He can take the item at the stated price or return it.
C) He must return the item and cannot keep it.
D) He can sue the seller for damages.

Key Takeaway
In murabahah, if the seller commits fraud by misstating the cost, the buyer may either accept the item at the stated price or return it, but cannot force a correction.

Truthful Cost Disclosure in Murabahah Sales
Ruling on correct wording for cost disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct wording for cost disclosure in a murabahah sale and explain why the alternative is impermissible.

Core Idea
Always say 'it cost me' not 'I bought it' to avoid lying about added expenses.

In murabahah (trust sale), the seller must honestly disclose the cost. This passage from al-Sharh al-Sameri gives a precise ruling: the seller should say 'قام على بكذا' (it stood me at such amount) rather than 'اشتريته بكذا' (I bought it for such amount). Why? Because the total cost includes additional expenses like wages, transport, etc. Claiming 'I bought it for X' when X includes those extras is a lie. The legal principle is that truthful dealing preserves trust in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of disclosing the cost in a murabahah sale. The ruling: it is obligatory (wajib) to use truthful wording; using a false statement is haram (unlawful) as it involves lying.
The text does not explicitly call it wajib, but the principle of truthfulness and prohibition of lying makes it so.
2
Recognize the correct vs. incorrect wording
Correct: 'قام على بكذا' (it cost me / it stood me at such amount). Incorrect: 'اشتريته بكذا' (I bought it for such amount). The difference is that the correct wording accounts for additional expenses added to the purchase price.
The Urdu commentary gives example: 'مجھ کو اتنی میں پڑی ہے' (it cost me such amount).
3
Understand the reason behind the ruling
If the seller adds expenses (e.g., wages, transport) to the principal price, then saying 'I bought it for that amount' is factually false because the original purchase price was lower. This constitutes lying, which is haram.
The underlying illa (effective cause) is the need to avoid falsehood and maintain trust.
4
Apply the general principle
The text states a general fiqh maxim: 'A person must always tell the truth to maintain trust.' This principle governs all transactions, especially trust-based sales like murabahah.
This is a qa'idah: truthfulness is an essential condition for the validity and barakah of transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) ويقول قام على بكذا ولا يقول اشتريته بكذا. اجرت وغیرہ جو کچھ اصل ثمن میں شامل کی جائے گی اس کو شامل کرنے کے بعد یہ نہ کہے کہ میں نے اتنے میں خریدی ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ تو جھوٹ ہو گا اتنے میں تو اس نے خریدی نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے یوں کہے کہ مجھے یہ میچ اتنے میں پڑی ہے۔ اصول آدمی ہر حال میں سچ بولے۔ تا کہ اعتماد بحال رہے۔ انتقام علی کذا : مجھ کو اتنی میں پڑی ہے۔
(5) And he should say 'it stood me at such amount' and should not say 'I bought it for such amount'. After adding expenses etc. into the principal price, he should not say 'I bought it for such amount', because that would be a lie, since he did not buy it for that amount. Therefore he should say 'this garment cost me such amount'. Principle: A person must always tell the truth to maintain trust. 'انتقام علی کذا' means 'it cost me such amount'.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding the wording used in a murabahah sale. In murabahah, the seller discloses his cost and adds a profit. The text instructs the seller to say 'قمت على كذا' (it stood me at such amount) rather than 'اشتريته بكذا' (I bought it for such amount). The reason is that the cost includes not only the purchase price but also additional expenses like wages and transport. Saying 'I bought it for such amount' would be inaccurate and a lie, especially if expenses are added. Therefore, the honest and precise statement is to mention the total cost incurred. The Urdu commentary emphasizes the principle of truthfulness to maintain trust in transactions.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.
قام على (qama 'ala): The Arabic phrase meaning 'it cost me' or 'it stood me at', used to disclose the actual expense incurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade saying 'ishtaraytuhu'.

Key Takeaway
Always use truthful wording ('it cost me') in murabahah to avoid lying and preserve transactional trust.

Buyer's Option Upon Discovering Fraud in Murabahah
Gives the buyer an option in case of fraudulent cost disclosure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the buyer's option in a murabahah sale when fraud is discovered, and explain the Hanafi ruling and its proof.

Core Idea
In a murabahah sale, if the seller lies about the cost, the buyer can only accept the full price or return—no forced reduction.

This lesson addresses the Hanafi ruling when a buyer discovers that the seller in a murabahah transaction lied about the original cost. Abu Hanifa gives the buyer a choice: take the item at the full stated price or return it. The key insight is that the murabahah contract remains valid, but the lie gives the buyer an option (khiyar). We will examine the ruling, its underlying principle, and the hadith proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
First, recognize this is about a murabahah sale (trust sale with disclosed cost and profit). The ruling is that the buyer has a khiyar (option) when fraud is discovered. The contract itself remains valid (sahih).
2
State the Hanafi option
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer may either take the item at the full stated price (including the false profit) or return it. He cannot unilaterally reduce the price to the true cost plus true profit.
This is the core practical consequence.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The seller's statement 'I am selling as murabahah' is truthful because the transaction is indeed a murabahah. The lie is only about the cost. Thus the buyer cannot invalidate the murabahah label but gets an option due to the deception.
The 'murabahah' word itself is truthful.
4
Examine the dalil
The proof is a hadith from Tirmidhi (from Abu Dharr) that condemns those who sell goods by false oath. While the hadith does not directly mention murabahah, it establishes the principle that falsehood in selling gives the buyer a right to rescind.
Hadith is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah, yet sufficient for a wajib ruling.
5
Note the practical consequence
In practice, the buyer cannot force a price adjustment. If both parties agree to a compromise, it is permissible but not enforceable. The ruling protects the seller's truthful statement of 'murabahah' while providing relief to the deceived buyer.
This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on the literal meaning of utterances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(6) If the buyer becomes aware of fraud in murabahah, he has the option, according to Abu Hanifa: if he wishes, he may take it at the full price, and if he wishes, he may return it. Then the Urdu explanation: If the buyer discovers fraud in a murabahah sale, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, he has the choice: either he can take it at the full price or return it. For example, he bought cloth for 10 pounds, and the seller fraudulently said he bought it for 12 pounds, and with 3 pounds profit sells for 15 pounds. The buyer trusted and bought, later found out the seller lied; he had bought it for 10 pounds and took 5 pounds profit instead of 3. According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer has the option to take or return. If he takes, he pays 15 pounds; he cannot legally take it for 13 pounds. Whether he takes for 13 or 15, it is still murabahah. The seller spoke the word 'murabahah' truthfully, so if purchased, it must be at 15. However, he lied about 2 pounds, so the buyer has the option. This ruling is based on the principle that the seller's word 'murabahah' is true; due to the lie, the buyer gets the option to reject. The hadith indicates this: from Abu Dharr from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Three people Allah will not look at on the Day of Judgment, nor will He purify them, and they will have a painful punishment.' We said: 'Who are they, O Messenger of Allah? They have indeed failed and lost.' He said: 'The one who reminds of favors, the one who lets his garment drag, and the one who sells his goods by false oath.' (Tirmidhi, Hadith no. 121). This hadith indicates that one who increases the price of his goods by false oath is punished by Allah. If both parties agree to reduce the false amount, they may do so, but the seller cannot be forced legally. Footnote: (A) The Prophet said: Three people Allah will not look at and will not purify and will have painful punishment. I asked who they are, and he said: the one who reminds of favors, the one who trails his garment, and the one who sells goods by false oath.

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the buyer's option when fraud (khayanah) is discovered in a murabahah transaction. According to Abu Hanifa, if the seller misrepresents the original cost, the buyer has the choice to either take the item at the full stated price (including the false profit) or return it. The key principle is that the term 'murabahah' itself is truthful as a label, but the underlying lie gives the buyer a right of rejection. The example illustrates: the seller bought for 10, claimed 12, and added 3 profit to sell at 15. The actual lie is in the cost, so the buyer cannot force a reduction to the true cost; he must either accept the 15 price or return. The proof is a hadith from Tirmidhi warning against false oaths in selling, which supports the idea that deception necessitates an option for the buyer. The Urdu commentary adds that if both parties agree to a compromise, it is permissible, but the seller cannot be compelled.

Key Terms
خيانة (khiyanah): Fraud or dishonesty, specifically lying about the original cost in a trust sale.
خيار (khiyar): The option or right to either continue or cancel a contract.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale with disclosed cost and profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Price Reduction for Fraud in Tawliyah
Ruling on deducting the false amount in a tawliyah sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's ruling on fraud in tawliyah and apply the principle of interpreting contract wording to distinguish it from murabahah.

Core Idea
In tawliyah, fraud is cured by deduction to true cost because the contract hinges on the word 'tawliyah' meaning actual cost.

This lesson examines a specific remedy for dishonesty in a tawliyah sale. Unlike murabahah where the buyer only has an option, in tawliyah the buyer can deduct the false amount and pay the actual cost. This is because the essence of tawliyah is transferring ownership at the original cost, so the seller's word binds him to that reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type and the fraud
Recognize that the sale is tawliyah (cost price) and the seller lied about the cost.
Tawliyah is a trust sale where the seller states the cost and transfers at that cost without profit.
2
Determine the Hanafi remedy
According to Abu Hanifa, the buyer deducts the false amount and pays the true cost.
The remedy is mandatory deduction, not a choice.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The contract's wording ('tawliyah') is interpreted literally: it implies selling at cost, so the buyer is entitled to the actual cost.
This contrasts with murabahah where the wording includes a declared profit margin.
4
Distinguish from murabahah
In murabahah, the seller declares a profit margin; the buyer cannot deduct but only has the option to take or return.
The difference stems from the nature of the contract: tawliyah is purely at cost, murabahah includes profit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٧) وان اطلع على خيانة في التولية اسقطها من الثمن . اور اگر خیانت پر مطلع ہوا بیع تولیہ میں تو ثمن میں سے اتنا کم کرے گا۔ مثلا دس پونڈ میں کپڑا خریدا تھا اور جھوٹ بولا کہ تیرہ پونڈ میں خریدا ہوں اور تیرہ پونڈ ہی پر تولیہ کرتا ہوں تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اس صورت میں تین پونڈ کم کر کے دس پونڈ ہی میں لے گا۔ تو یہ کہتے ہیں اس بیع کو کہ جتنے میں خریدا ہے اتنے میں ہی دوں گا اور حقیقت میں دس پونڈ ہی میں خریدا تھا۔ جھوٹ بولا تھا کہ تیرہ پونڈ میں خریدا تھا۔ اس لئے جتنے میں خریدا تھا اتنے ہی میں مشتری لے گا۔ یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جو لفظ تولیہ بولا ہے اس کی حقیقت پر فیصلہ کیا جائے گا اور اسی قیمت پر مشتری کو لینے کا حق ہوگا۔ اور وہ ہے کم قیمت یعنی دس پونڈ ۔ حدیث او پر گزر چکی ہے۔ اصول بیع مرابحہ اور تولیہ کا مدار ان کے الفاظ پر ہوگا۔
(7) And if he becomes aware of fraud in tawliyah, he deducts it from the price. If he discovers fraud in a tawliyah sale, he will deduct that amount from the price. For example, he bought cloth for 10 pounds, and the seller falsely claimed he bought it for 13 pounds, and offers tawliyah at 13. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in this case the buyer deducts 3 pounds and takes it for 10 pounds. Because the seller says 'I give it to you at the cost I bought it,' and in reality he bought for 10. He lied saying he bought for 13. Therefore the buyer takes it at the actual cost. This ruling is based on the principle that the word 'tawliyah' is interpreted according to its reality, and the buyer has the right to take at the true cost, which is the lower price, i.e., 10 pounds. The hadith has passed previously. Principle: The sales of murabahah and tawliyah are based on their wording.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hanifa's ruling on tawliyah (a sale at cost without profit) when fraud is discovered. The key difference from murabahah is that in tawliyah, the seller explicitly states that he is selling at cost. Therefore, if he lies about the cost, the buyer is entitled to deduct the false amount and pay only the true cost. In the example, the seller said the cost was 13 but actually it was 10; the buyer thus pays 10. The underlying principle is that the contract is interpreted according to its wording: 'tawliyah' implies cost price, so the buyer should receive the actual cost. The earlier hadith about false oaths applies here as well. This contrasts with murabahah, where the buyer cannot deduct but only take or leave.

Key Terms
تولية (tawliyah): A sale where the seller transfers ownership at the original cost without profit.
إسقاط (isqat): Deduction or removal; here, deducting the false amount from the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the remedy for fraud in a tawliyah sale?
A) The buyer has the option to take or return
B) The buyer deducts the false amount and pays the true cost
C) The sale is void
D) The buyer must pay the stated price

Key Takeaway
In tawliyah, the buyer deducts the false amount and pays the true cost because the contract is interpreted literally as a sale at cost.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Fraud Remedy in Murabahah and Tawliyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fraud remedy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the remedy for fraud in murabahah and tawliyah sales, and explain the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two views.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf cuts the price; Muhammad cuts the contract — same fraud, two Hanafi remedies.

This passage presents a disagreement between two giants of the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, on how to handle a seller's lie in a murabahah or tawliyah transaction. Abu Yusuf focuses on the deceit itself and orders a price reduction, while Muhammad insists the contract price is binding but gives the buyer an option to cancel. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between remedies that modify the contract versus those that terminate it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting positions
Note that Imam Abu Yusuf rules for price deduction in both murabahah and tawliyah, while Imam Muhammad rules for no deduction but gives the buyer an option (khiyar) to rescind.
The dispute is about the remedy for fraud, not about the validity of the contract itself.
2
Understand the underlying principle of each
Abu Yusuf's principle: the buyer should not pay for the false increment; the lie directly affects the price. Muhammad's principle: the agreed price is binding; the terms 'murabahah' and 'tawliyah' are mere inducements, but the lie gives a right to cancel.
Muhammad treats the fraud as a defect in consent, not a defect in price.
3
Apply the example
If the seller bought for 10, said it was 13, and sold at cost-plus (murabahah) for 15, then under Abu Yusuf the buyer pays 12 (10 + 2 profit, with 3 deducted). Under Muhammad the buyer must pay 15 but may return the item.
The subtraction is of the false amount (3) plus any profit based on that false amount.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical outcome: under Abu Yusuf the buyer gets a reduced price; under Muhammad the buyer either pays the full price or cancels. Thus the financial burden differs significantly.
This difference affects both parties' expectations and the final transaction value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٨) وقال ابو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى يحط فيهما وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى لا يحط فيهما لكن يخير فيهما. اور امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا دونوں صورتوں میں کم کیا جائے گا، اور امام محمد نے فرمایا دونوں صورتوں میں کم نہیں کیا جائے گا لیکن دونوں کو اختیار دیا جائے گا۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ تولیہ اور مرابحہ دونوں صورتوں میں جتنی قیمت جھوٹ بول کر لی ہے اتنی قیمت کم کر کے مشتری کو لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ مثلا مثال مذکور میں دس پونڈ میں خریدا تھا اور جھوٹ بولا تھا کہ تیرہ پونڈ میں خریدا ہے تو تین پونڈ جھوٹ بول کر لئے تھے اس لئے مرابحہ اور تولیہ دونوں صورتوں میں تین پونڈ کم کر کے لے گا۔ اس لئے مرابحہ کی شکل میں پندرہ کی بجائے بارہ پونڈ دے گا اور تولیہ کی شکل میں دس پونڈ ہی دے گا۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ مرابحہ اور تولیہ دونوں صورتوں میں کم نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ بائع نے ترغیب دینے کے لئے مرابحہ اور تولیہ کی بات کی ہے۔ اصل مقصود تو وہ قیمت ہے جس پر بات طے ہوئی ہے۔ بائع مرابحہ میں مثلا پندرہ پونڈ اور تولیہ میں مثلا تیرہ پونڈ سے کم پر دینے کے لئے راضی نہیں ہے۔ اور اس پر بات بھی طے ہوئی ہے اس لئے اس سے کم نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ البتہ چونکہ بائع جھوٹ بولا ہے اس لئے مشتری کو لینے نہ لینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ انکا اصول یہ ہے کہ جس قیمت پر بات طے ہوئی ہے وہی لازم ہوگی۔ مرابحہ اور تولیہ کا لفظ تر غیب کے لئے ہے۔ انت محط : کم کیا جائے گا مشتق ہے حط سے کم کرنا۔
(8) And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said that it is deducted in both cases, and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said that it is not deducted in both cases, but an option is given in both. Abu Yusuf ruled that in both tawliyah and murabahah, whatever amount was taken by lying will be deducted, and the buyer will have the right to take at that reduced price. In the example, if bought for 10 and lied that it was 13, the buyer deducts 3, paying 12 in murabahah (instead of 15) and 10 in tawliyah. Muhammad ruled that there is no deduction in either case. The seller used the terms murabahah and tawliyah as encouragement. The actual intended price is the agreed amount; the seller is not willing to sell for less. Hence no deduction, but due to the lie, the buyer has an option to take or leave. Their principle is that the agreed price is binding, and the words murabahah/tawliyah are mere inducements. 'Mahatt' (deduction) is derived from 'hatt' (to decrease).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the remedy for fraud in murabahah and tawliyah sales. Abu Yusuf holds that in both cases, the price should be reduced by the amount of the false statement. For example, if the seller lied about 3 pounds, the buyer pays 12 instead of 15 in murabahah and 10 instead of 13 in tawliyah. Muhammad holds that there should be no reduction; the buyer must pay the full agreed price, but he gets an option to cancel the sale because of the lie. Their principles differ: Abu Yusuf focuses on the lie itself (the buyer should not pay for the false amount), while Muhammad focuses on the contract terms (the agreed price is binding, but the buyer can rescind due to deception). The text notes that the word 'tawliyah' and 'murabahah' are mere inducements in Muhammad's view. The three opinions (Abu Hanifa's earlier, plus these two) represent the spectrum of early Hanafi thought on this issue.

Key Terms
يحط (yahutt): He deducts; from 'hatt' (deduction). Used for price reduction.
يخير (yukhayyar): He gives an option (to the buyer to accept or reject).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, what is the buyer's remedy upon discovering fraud in a murabahah sale?
A) The buyer deducts the false amount
B) The buyer pays the stated price but may return the item
C) The buyer pays the true cost
D) The sale is automatically void

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two valid remedies for fraud in murabahah/tawliyah: price reduction (Abu Yusuf) or option to rescind (Muhammad); the mufta bihi is the latter.

Prohibition of Selling Movable Goods Before Possession
Ruling with evidence: prohibition of pre-possession sale for movables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-possession sale of movables and identify the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Selling a movable good before taking possession is prohibited (haram) due to the hadith and to prevent gharar.

This passage establishes a fundamental principle in Hanafi sales law: the buyer of a movable item cannot resell it until he has physically or constructively taken possession. The ruling is grounded in the prophetic command not to sell what one does not possess. Understanding this rule helps prevent uncertainty in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling a movable good before possession. The ruling is 'not permissible' (haram).
The Arabic 'لم يجز' indicates prohibition.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence is the hadith: 'Do not sell what is not with you' (لا تبع ما ليس عندك), reported by Abu Dawud.
The hadith is a sound (hasan) hadith commonly cited in sales.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'لا تبع' indicates prohibition; 'ما ليس عندك' includes goods not yet possessed.
In Hanafi usul, the imperative form (amr) generally indicates obligation but here it is negated, indicating prohibition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
All major schools agree on this prohibition for movables, though exceptions exist for salam (advance payment) and real estate.
This passage focuses on movables; immovables may have different rulings.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is the strict prohibition for movables, as per the zahir al-riwayah.
The ruling is consistent across the Hanafi corpus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٩) ومن اشترى شيئا مما ينقل ويحول لم يجز له بيعه حتى يقبضه . کسی نے کوئی ایسی چیز خریدی جو منتقل ہو سکتی ہے تو اس کی بیع جائز نہیں ہے جب تک اس پر قبضہ نہ کر لے۔ منتقل ہونے والی چیز پر قبضہ کرے تب اس کو آگے بیچے۔ کیونکہ قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بیچے گا تو ہوسکتا ہے کہ وہ چیز ضائع ہو جائے اور اس کے پاس نہ آئے تو کیسے بیچے گا (۲) پہلے حدیث گزر چکی ہے جو چیز تمہارے پاس نہ ہو اس کو نہ بیچو، لاتبع ماليس عندک (ابوداؤد شریف
(9) And whoever buys something that can be moved and transferred, it is not permissible for him to sell it until he takes possession of it. If someone buys a moveable item, selling it is not permissible until he takes possession. He should take possession of the moveable item and then sell it further. Because if he sells before taking possession, it is possible that the item may be lost and not reach him, so how will he sell it? (2) The hadith has passed earlier: 'Do not sell what is not with you; do not sell what you do not possess.' (Abu Dawud)
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage gives a fundamental rule in sales: a buyer cannot resell a movable good until he has taken possession (qabd). The reason is that until delivery, the risk of loss remains with the seller, and the buyer does not have full ownership necessary to transfer title. The proof is the hadith: 'Do not sell what is not with you' (la tabi' ma laysa 'indak). This hadith is cited in Abu Dawud. The rule applies to movable items that can be transported (manqul wa muhawwal), as opposed to immovable property (like land) which may have different rules. The wisdom is to prevent gharar (uncertainty) and ensure the seller can deliver what he sells.

Key Terms
مما ينقل ويحول (mimma yanqalu wa yahulu): Movable goods that can be transported; excludes immovable property.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive possession; taking delivery of the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only after taking possession
C) After one day
D) After paying the price

Key Takeaway
A buyer must physically or constructively possess a movable good before reselling it, based on the hadith and to avoid gharar.

Deduction for Fraud in Bay' al-Tawliyah
Ruling on deducting fraudulent excess in cost sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fraud in a tawliyah sale and apply the corrective measure (price reduction) to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Fraud in cost sale does not void the contract; the buyer pays the true cost.

In a tawliyah (cost-price) sale, the seller transfers the item at the price he paid. If he inflates the cost, the buyer might be misled. This lesson explains the Hanafi remedy: the fraudulent excess is deducted, and the buyer takes the item at the actual price. This preserves the contract's validity while correcting the price to reflect truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Selling at cost (tawliyah) and the occurrence of fraud (khiyanah). The ruling: the contract remains valid, but the price is reduced to the actual cost paid by the seller.
2
Understand the reason for correction
Why does the price get reduced? Because the word 'tawliyah' literally means 'I give it to you at my cost'. The seller's false claim (13 pounds) contradicts the reality (10 pounds). The contract is interpreted according to its literal meaning, so the buyer only owes the true cost.
The principle: contracts are construed according to their literal wording (al-‘ibrah fi al-‘uqud lil-ma‘ani la lil-alfaz? Actually here it's literal meaning).
3
Apply to a concrete example
Take the given example: seller bought cloth for 10, claimed 13, made tawliyah at 13. The ruling: buyer deducts 3 and pays 10. Verify the result matches the principle.
Numerical example from the text.
4
Note the absence of option
The buyer is not given an option (khiyar) to cancel the sale; he must take it at the true cost. This is because the contract is valid and only the price is corrected.
Contrast with khiyar al-ghabn (option for gross overcharging) in other sales.

And if he discovers fraud in tawliyah, he deducts it from the price. And if he discovers fraud in the sale of tawliyah, then he will deduct that amount from the price. For example, he bought cloth for ten pounds and lied saying he bought it for thirteen pounds, and he does tawliyah on thirteen pounds. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, he will deduct three pounds and take it for ten pounds. He (Imam Abu Hanifa) says: I will give it to you at the price I bought it, and in reality it was ten pounds. He lied that he bought it for thirteen pounds. Therefore, the buyer will take it at the price he bought it, which is the lower price, i.e., ten pounds. Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding fraud in a sale of tawliyah (sale at cost price). The ruling states: If the seller fraudulently claims a higher purchase price than he actually paid, the fraudulent excess is deducted from the price, and the buyer takes the item at the true cost. For example, if the seller bought cloth for 10 pounds but claimed he bought for 13 and made a tawliyah contract at 13, then the buyer pays only 10 pounds.
